   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Potential energy
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <table class="infobox" style="width:22em">
       <tr>
        <th colspan="2" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;font-style:italic;">
         Potential energy
        </th>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <td colspan="2" style="text-align:center">
         <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mediaeval_archery_reenactment.jpg">
          <img alt="Mediaeval archery reenactment.jpg" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="480" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Mediaeval_archery_reenactment.jpg/220px-Mediaeval_archery_reenactment.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Mediaeval_archery_reenactment.jpg/330px-Mediaeval_archery_reenactment.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Mediaeval_archery_reenactment.jpg/440px-Mediaeval_archery_reenactment.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
         </a>
         <div>
          In the case of a
          <a href="/wiki/Bow_and_arrow" title="Bow and arrow">
           bow and arrow
          </a>
          , when the archer does work on the bow, drawing the string back, some of the chemical energy of the archer's body is transformed into elastic potential energy in the bent limbs of the bow. When the string is released, the force between the string and the arrow does work on the arrow. The potential energy in the bow limbs is transformed into the
          <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">
           kinetic energy
          </a>
          of the arrow as it takes flight.
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">
          Common symbols
         </div>
        </th>
        <td>
         <i>
          PE
         </i>
         ,
         <i>
          U
         </i>
         , or
         <i>
          V
         </i>
        </td>
       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">

         (J)

       </tr>
       <tr>
        <th scope="row">
         <div style="padding:0.1em 0;line-height:1.2em;">

        </th>
        <td>

          <i>

          <i>

            g
           </a>
          </i>
          ·
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Height" title="Height">
            h
           </a>
          </i>
          (
          <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential_energy" title="Gravitational potential energy">
           gravitational
          </a>
          )
          <br/>
          <i>
           U
          </i>
          = ½ ·
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Hooke%27s_law" title="Hooke's law">
            k
           </a>
          </i>
          ·
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Stress_(mechanics)" title="Stress (mechanics)">
            x
           </a>
          </i>
          <sup>
           2
          </sup>
          <i>
           U
          </i>
          = ½ ·
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Capacitance" title="Capacitance">
            C
           </a>
          </i>
          ·
          <i>
           <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential" title="Electric potential">
            V
           </a>
          </i>
          <sup>
           2
          </sup>
          (
          <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential_energy" title="Electric potential energy">
           electric
          </a>
          )
          <br/>
         </p>
         <i>
          U
         </i>
         = -
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">
           m
          </a>
         </i>
         ·
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
           B
          </a>
         </i>
         (
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magnetic_potential_energy" title="Magnetic potential energy">
          magnetic
         </a>
         )

       <tr>
        <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;padding-top:0.15em;">
         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
          <ul>
           <li class="nv-view">
            <a href="/wiki/Template:Classical_mechanics" title="Template:Classical mechanics">
             <abbr title="View this template">
              v
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-talk">
            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Classical_mechanics" title="Template talk:Classical mechanics">
             <abbr title="Discuss this template">
              t
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li class="nv-edit">
            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Classical_mechanics&amp;action=edit">
             <abbr title="Edit this template">
              e
             </abbr>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </td>
       </tr>
      </table>
      <p>
       In physics,
       <b>
        potential energy
       </b>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">
        energy
       </a>
       possessed by an object because of its position relative to other objects, stresses within itself, its electric charge, or other factors.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Common types of potential energy include the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential_energy" title="Gravitational potential energy">
        gravitational potential energy
       </a>
       of an object that depends on its
       <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">
        mass
       </a>
       and its distance from the center of mass of another object, the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elastic_potential_energy" title="Elastic potential energy">
        elastic potential energy
       </a>
       of an extended spring, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential_energy" title="Electric potential energy">
        electric potential energy
       </a>
       of an
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
        electric charge
       </a>
       in an
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_field" title="Electric field">
        electric field
       </a>
       . The unit for energy in the
       <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">
        International System of Units
       </a>
       (SI) is the
       <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">
        joule
       </a>
       , which has the symbol
       <i>
        J
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The term
       <i>
        potential energy
       </i>
       was introduced by the 19th century Scottish engineer and physicist
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/William_Rankine" title="William Rankine">
        William Rankine
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       although it has links to Greek philosopher
       <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">
        Aristotle
       </a>
       's concept of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potentiality_and_Actuality" title="Potentiality and Actuality">
        potentiality
       </a>
       . Potential energy is associated with forces that act on a body in a way that the total work done by these forces on the body depends only on the initial and final positions of the body in space. These forces, that are called
       <i>
        conservative forces
       </i>
       , can be represented at every point in space by vectors expressed as gradients of a certain scalar function called
       <i>
        potential
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Since the work of potential forces acting on a body that moves from a start to an end position is determined only by these two positions, and does not depend on the trajectory of the body, there is a function known as
       <i>
        potential
       </i>
       or
       <i>
        potential energy
       </i>
       that can be evaluated at the two positions to determine this work.
      </p>
      <p>
      </p>

      <p>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">
        Overview
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       There are various types of potential energy, each associated with a particular type of force. For example, the work of an
       <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">
        elastic
       </a>
       force is called elastic potential energy; work of the gravitational force is called gravitational potential energy; work of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coulomb_force" title="Coulomb force">
        Coulomb force
       </a>
       is called
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential_energy" title="Electric potential energy">
        electric potential energy
       </a>
       ; work of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_nuclear_force" title="Strong nuclear force">
        strong nuclear force
       </a>
       or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_nuclear_force" title="Weak nuclear force">
        weak nuclear force
       </a>
       acting on the
       <a href="/wiki/Baryon" title="Baryon">
        baryon
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Charge_(physics)" title="Charge (physics)">
        charge
       </a>
       is called nuclear potential energy; work of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intermolecular_forces" title="Intermolecular forces">
        intermolecular forces
       </a>
       is called intermolecular potential energy. Chemical potential energy, such as the energy stored in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" title="Fossil fuels">
        fossil fuels
       </a>
       , is the work of the Coulomb force during rearrangement of mutual positions of electrons and nuclei in atoms and molecules. Thermal energy usually has two components: the kinetic energy of random motions of particles and the potential energy of their mutual positions.
      </p>
      <p>
       Forces derivable from a potential are also called
       <a href="/wiki/Conservative_force" title="Conservative force">
        conservative forces
       </a>
       . The work done by a conservative force is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \,W=-\Delta U}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mspace width="thinmathspace">
              </mspace>
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               Δ
               <!-- Δ -->
              </mi>
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \,W=-\Delta U}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\,W = -\Delta U" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/67b56bb6af9e1ce117dc15642c46073a53575678" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:11.447ex; height:2.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \Delta U}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi mathvariant="normal">
              Δ
              <!-- Δ -->
             </mi>
             <mi>
              U
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \Delta U}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\Delta U" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d4716a2c49bbbe155e8b399117ca78342e802cbe" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:3.718ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       is the change in the potential energy associated with the force. The negative sign provides the convention that work done against a force field increases potential energy, while work done by the force field decreases potential energy. Common notations for potential energy are
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       ,
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       , also
       <i>
        E
        <sub>
         p
        </sub>
       </i>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Potential energy is the energy by virtue of an object's position relative to other objects.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Potential energy is often associated with restoring
       <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">
        forces
       </a>
       such as a
       <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">
        spring
       </a>
       or the force of
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity" title="Gravity">
        gravity
       </a>
       . The action of stretching a spring or lifting a mass is performed by an external force that works against the force field of the potential. This work is stored in the force field, which is said to be stored as potential energy. If the external force is removed the force field acts on the body to perform the work as it moves the body back to the initial position, reducing the stretch of the spring or causing a body to fall.
      </p>
      <p>
       Suppose a ball which mass is m, and it is in h position in height. If the acceleration of free fall is g, the weight of the ball is mg. So the total work or energy is force × displacement = mg × h = mgh
       <br/>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle E_{p}=mgh}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                E
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 p
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mi>
               g
              </mi>
              <mi>
               h
              </mi>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle E_{p}=mgh}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="E_{p}=mgh" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/71be3ed0d4d97cf9428feafafc7b7f41b3326038" style="vertical-align: -1.005ex; width:10.368ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The more formal definition is that potential energy is the energy difference between the energy of an object in a given position and its energy at a reference position.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Work_and_potential_energy">
        Work and potential energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Work and potential energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Potential energy is closely linked with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Force_(physics)" title="Force (physics)">
        forces
       </a>
       . If the work done by a force on a body that moves from
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        B
       </i>
       does not depend on the path between these points, then the work of this force measured from
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       assigns a scalar value to every other point in space and defines a
       <a href="/wiki/Scalar_potential" title="Scalar potential">
        scalar potential
       </a>
       field. In this case, the force can be defined as the negative of the
       <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">
        vector gradient
       </a>
       of the potential field.
      </p>
      <p>
       If the work for an applied force is independent of the path, then the work done by the force is evaluated at the start and end of the trajectory of the point of application. This means that there is a function
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       (
       <b>
        x
       </b>
       ), called a "potential," that can be evaluated at the two points
       <b>
        x
       </b>
       <sub>
        A
       </sub>
       and
       <b>
        x
       </b>
       <sub>
        B
       </sub>
       to obtain the work over any trajectory between these two points. It is tradition to define this function with a negative sign so that positive work is a reduction in the potential, that is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle W=\int _{C}{\mathbf {F} }\cdot \mathrm {d} {\mathbf {x} }=U(\mathbf {x} _{A})-U(\mathbf {x} _{B})}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mo>
                ∫
                <!-- ∫ -->
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 C
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="bold">
                 F
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="bold">
                 x
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="bold">
                 x
                </mi>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 A
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="bold">
                 x
                </mi>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 B
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle W=\int _{C}{\mathbf {F} }\cdot \mathrm {d} {\mathbf {x} }=U(\mathbf {x} _{A})-U(\mathbf {x} _{B})}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="W =\int_{C} \bold{F} \cdot \mathrm{d}\bold{x} =  U(\mathbf{x}_A)-U(\mathbf{x}_B)" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/70c971c38c1e4eca0c3fb3b1e024b88be3eca9c9" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:33.649ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        C
       </i>
       is the trajectory taken from A to B. Because the work done is independent of the path taken, then this expression is true for any trajectory,
       <i>
        C
       </i>
       , from A to B.
      </p>
      <p>
       The function
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       (
       <b>
        x
       </b>
       ) is called the potential energy associated with the applied force. Examples of forces that have potential energies are gravity and spring forces.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Derivable_from_a_potential">
        Derivable from a potential
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Derivable from a potential">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In this section the relationship between work and potential energy is presented in more detail. The
       <a href="/wiki/Line_integral" title="Line integral">
        line integral
       </a>
       that defines work along curve
       <i>
        C
       </i>
       takes a special form if the force
       <b>
        F
       </b>
       is related to a scalar field φ(
       <b>
        x
       </b>
       ) so that
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} ={\nabla \varphi }=\left({\frac {\partial \varphi }{\partial x}},{\frac {\partial \varphi }{\partial y}},{\frac {\partial \varphi }{\partial z}}\right).}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>

            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} ={\nabla \varphi }=\left({\frac {\partial \varphi }{\partial x}},{\frac {\partial \varphi }{\partial y}},{\frac {\partial \varphi }{\partial z}}\right).}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" \mathbf{F}={\nabla \varphi} = \left ( \frac{\partial \varphi}{\partial x}, \frac{\partial \varphi}{\partial y}, \frac{\partial \varphi}{\partial z} \right ).         </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In this case, work along the curve is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle W=\int _{C}{\mathbf {F}}\cdot \mathrm {d} {\mathbf {x}}=\int _{C}\nabla \varphi \cdot \mathrm {d} {\mathbf {x}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

      </p>

              </mi>

            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" W= \varphi(\mathbf{x}_B) - \varphi(\mathbf{x}_A)." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/affa69cc8d6cf0e12c57bfd976e82ae447d849e9" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:21.446ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       This shows that when forces are derivable from a scalar field, the work of those forces along a curve
       <i>
        C
       </i>
       is computed by evaluating the scalar field at the start point
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       and the end point
       <i>
        B
       </i>
       of the curve. This means the work integral does not depend on the path between
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        B
       </i>
       and is said to be independent of the path.
      </p>
      <p>
       Potential energy
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       =-φ(
       <b>
        x
       </b>
       ) is traditionally defined as the negative of this scalar field so that work by the force field decreases potential energy, that is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle W=U(\mathbf {x} _{A})-U(\mathbf {x} _{B}).}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>

              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="bold">
                 x
                </mi>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 A
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi>

              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">

                <mi>
                 B
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo stretchy="false">

              </mo>
              <mo>

              </mo>

            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle W=U(\mathbf {x} _{A})-U(\mathbf {x} _{B}).}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" W= U(\mathbf{x}_A) - U(\mathbf{x}_B)." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/1f48a19cb05ea5e2641d28e8987736d63e7bfc22" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:21.971ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       In this case, the application of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Del_operator" title="Del operator">
        del operator
       </a>
       to the work function yields,
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">

          <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\nabla W}=-{\nabla U}=-\left({\frac {\partial U}{\partial x}},{\frac {\partial U}{\partial y}},{\frac {\partial U}{\partial z}}\right)=\mathbf {F} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

              </mo>

                    <!-- ∂ -->
                   </mi>
                   <mi>

                  </mrow>
                 </mfrac>
                </mrow>
                <mo>
                 ,
                </mo>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mfrac>
                  <mrow>
                   <mi mathvariant="normal">
                    ∂
                    <!-- ∂ -->
                   </mi>
                   <mi>
                    U
                   </mi>
                  </mrow>
                  <mrow>
                   <mi mathvariant="normal">

                  </mrow>
                 </mfrac>

                <mo>

                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

                  <mrow>
                   <mi mathvariant="normal">

                   </mi>

                 </mfrac>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
               <mo>
                )
               </mo>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\nabla W}=-{\nabla U}=-\left({\frac {\partial U}{\partial x}},{\frac {\partial U}{\partial y}},{\frac {\partial U}{\partial z}}\right)=\mathbf {F} ,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" {\nabla W} =  -{\nabla U} = -\left ( \frac{\partial U}{\partial x}, \frac{\partial U}{\partial y}, \frac{\partial U}{\partial z} \right ) = \mathbf{F}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6eadee4df78e6e0a7163cc581adbe3e8fb31c433" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:41.017ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       and the force
       <b>
        F
       </b>
       is said to be "derivable from a potential."
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This also necessarily implies that
       <b>
        F
       </b>
       must be a
       <a href="/wiki/Conservative_vector_field" title="Conservative vector field">
        conservative vector field
       </a>
       . The potential
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       defines a force
       <b>
        F
       </b>
       at every point
       <b>
        x
       </b>
       in space, so the set of forces is called a
       <a href="/wiki/Force_field_(physics)" title="Force field (physics)">
        force field
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Computing_potential_energy">
        Computing potential energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Computing potential energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Given a force field
       <b>
        F
       </b>
       (
       <b>
        x
       </b>
       ), evaluation of the work integral using the
       <a href="/wiki/Gradient_theorem" title="Gradient theorem">
        gradient theorem
       </a>
       can be used to find the scalar function associated with potential energy. This is done by introducing a parameterized curve γ(t)=
       <b>
        r
       </b>
       (t) from γ(a)=A to γ(b)=B, and computing,
      </p>

       <dd>

      <p>
       The power applied to a body by a force field is obtained from the gradient of the work, or potential, in the direction of the velocity
       <b>
        v
       </b>
       of the point of application, that is
      </p>

               </mi>
               <mi>
                U
               </mi>

              </mo>

            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="P(t) = -{\nabla U} \cdot \mathbf{v} = \mathbf{F}\cdot\mathbf{v}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c83c632670f20b5c035568a8d32c5eafb4d24cbf" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:24.628ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>

      <p>
       Examples of work that can be computed from potential functions are gravity and spring forces.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_energy_for_near_Earth_gravity">
        Potential energy for near Earth gravity
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Potential energy for near Earth gravity">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Trebuchet.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="600" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Trebuchet.jpg/220px-Trebuchet.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Trebuchet.jpg/330px-Trebuchet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Trebuchet.jpg/440px-Trebuchet.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Trebuchet.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         A
         <a href="/wiki/Trebuchet" title="Trebuchet">
          trebuchet
         </a>
         uses the gravitational potential energy of the
         <a href="/wiki/Counterweight" title="Counterweight">
          counterweight
         </a>
         to throw projectiles over two hundred metres
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The following function is called the potential energy of a near earth gravity field:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U(\mathbf {r} )=mg\,h(\mathbf {r} ),}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                r
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mi>
               g
              </mi>
              <mspace width="thinmathspace">
              </mspace>
              <mi>
               h
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                r
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U(\mathbf {r} )=mg\,h(\mathbf {r} ),}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="U({\mathbf  {r}})=mg\,h({\mathbf  {r}})," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cd3ff1d27fb6676b2fe56ba318a3b7e40f8e30a9" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:16.233ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where m is in kg, g is 9.81 for earth and h is in metres.
      </p>
      <p>
       In classical physics, gravity exerts a constant downward force
       <i>
        <b>
         F
        </b>
       </i>
       =(0, 0,
       <i>
        F
        <sub>
         z
        </sub>
       </i>
       ) on the center of mass of a body moving near the surface of the Earth. The work of gravity on a body moving along a trajectory
       <i>
        <b>
         r
        </b>
       </i>
       (t) = (
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       (t),
       <i>
        y
       </i>
       (t),
       <i>
        z
       </i>
       (t)), such as the track of a roller coaster is calculated using its velocity,
       <i>
        <b>
         v
        </b>
       </i>
       =(
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        x
       </sub>
       ,
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        y
       </sub>
       ,
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        z
       </sub>
       ), to obtain
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle W=\int _{t_{1}}^{t_{2}}{\boldsymbol {F}}\cdot {\boldsymbol {v}}\mathrm {d} t=\int _{t_{1}}^{t_{2}}F_{z}v_{z}\mathrm {d} t=F_{z}\Delta z.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msubsup>
               <mo>
                ∫
                <!-- ∫ -->
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   1
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msub>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msub>
               </mrow>
              </msubsup>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                v
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msubsup>
               <mo>
                ∫
                <!-- ∫ -->
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   1
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msub>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <msub>
                 <mi>
                  t
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msub>
               </mrow>
              </msubsup>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                F
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 z
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                v
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 z
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                F
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 z
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               Δ
               <!-- Δ -->
              </mi>
              <mi>
               z
              </mi>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle W=\int _{t_{1}}^{t_{2}}{\boldsymbol {F}}\cdot {\boldsymbol {v}}\mathrm {d} t=\int _{t_{1}}^{t_{2}}F_{z}v_{z}\mathrm {d} t=F_{z}\Delta z.}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="W=\int_{t_1}^{t_2}\boldsymbol{F}\cdot\boldsymbol{v}\mathrm{d}t = \int_{t_1}^{t_2}F_z v_z \mathrm{d}t = F_z\Delta z. " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/f4046760fd3f62ff48eb4cae008ad752418fed44" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:40.682ex; height:6.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the integral of the vertical component of velocity is the vertical distance. Notice that the work of gravity depends only on the vertical movement of the curve
       <i>
        <b>
         r
        </b>
       </i>
       (t).
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_energy_for_a_linear_spring">
        Potential energy for a linear spring
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Potential energy for a linear spring">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elastic_potential_energy" title="Elastic potential energy">
        Elastic potential energy
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Springs_009.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1252" data-file-width="1836" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Springs_009.jpg/220px-Springs_009.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Springs_009.jpg/330px-Springs_009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Springs_009.jpg/440px-Springs_009.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Springs_009.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">
          Springs
         </a>
         are used for storing
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elastic_potential_energy" title="Elastic potential energy">
          elastic potential energy
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Longbowmen.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="433" data-file-width="394" height="242" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Longbowmen.jpg/220px-Longbowmen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Longbowmen.jpg/330px-Longbowmen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/a/a7/Longbowmen.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Longbowmen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">
          Archery
         </a>
         is one of humankind's oldest applications of elastic potential energy
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       A horizontal spring exerts a force
       <b>
        F
       </b>
       = (−
       <i>
        kx
       </i>
       , 0, 0) that is proportional to its deflection in the
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       direction. The work of this spring on a body moving along the space curve
       <b>
        s
       </b>
       (
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ) = (
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        y
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       ),
       <i>
        z
       </i>
       (
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       )), is calculated using its velocity,
       <b>
        v
       </b>
       = (
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        x
       </sub>
       ,
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        y
       </sub>
       ,
       <i>
        v
       </i>
       <sub>
        z
       </sub>
       ), to obtain
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle W=\int _{0}^{t}\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {v} \mathrm {\,} {d}t=-\int _{0}^{t}kxv_{x}\mathrm {\,} {d}t=-{\frac {1}{2}}kx^{2}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               W
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <msubsup>
               <mo>
                ∫
                <!-- ∫ -->
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 0
                </mn>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 t
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msubsup>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                v
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mspace width="thinmathspace">
               </mspace>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <msubsup>
               <mo>
                ∫
                <!-- ∫ -->
               </mo>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 0
                </mn>
               </mrow>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 t
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msubsup>
              <mi>
               k
              </mi>
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                v
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 x
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mspace width="thinmathspace">
               </mspace>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               t
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               k
              </mi>
              <msup>
               <mi>
                x
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle W=\int _{0}^{t}\mathbf {F} \cdot \mathbf {v} \mathrm {\,} {d}t=-\int _{0}^{t}kxv_{x}\mathrm {\,} {d}t=-{\frac {1}{2}}kx^{2}.}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="W=\int_0^t\mathbf{F}\cdot\mathbf{v}\mathrm\,{d}t =-\int_0^t kx v_x \mathrm\,{d}t = -\frac{1}{2}kx^2. " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/be117a613f1292e44d36ffa23b5ba23da2d17ba6" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:43.828ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       For convenience, consider contact with the spring occurs at
       <i>
        t
       </i>
       = 0, then the integral of the product of the distance
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       and the
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       -velocity,
       <i>
        xv
        <sub>
         x
        </sub>
       </i>
       , is
       <i>
        x
       </i>
       <sup>
        2
       </sup>
       /2.
      </p>
      <p>
       The function
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U(x)={\frac {1}{2}}kx^{2},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mi>
               x
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               k
              </mi>
              <msup>
               <mi>
                x
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mrow>
              </msup>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U(x)={\frac {1}{2}}kx^{2},}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" U(x) = \frac{1}{2}kx^2," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4c7e243bdec3b25a115573033e700cdc4cb01505" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:14.261ex; height:5.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       is called the potential energy of a linear spring.
      </p>
      <p>
       Elastic potential energy is the potential energy of an
       <a href="/wiki/Elasticity_(physics)" title="Elasticity (physics)">
        elastic
       </a>
       object (for example a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bow_(weapon)" title="Bow (weapon)">
        bow
       </a>
       or a catapult) that is deformed under tension or compression (or
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(physics)" title="Stress (physics)">
        stressed
       </a>
       in formal terminology). It arises as a consequence of a force that tries to restore the object to its original shape, which is most often the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_force" title="Electromagnetic force">
        electromagnetic force
       </a>
       between the atoms and molecules that constitute the object. If the stretch is released, the energy is transformed into
       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">
        kinetic energy
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_energy_for_gravitational_forces_between_two_bodies">
        Potential energy for gravitational forces between two bodies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Potential energy for gravitational forces between two bodies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The gravitational potential function, also known as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential_energy" title="Gravitational potential energy">
        gravitational potential energy
       </a>
       , is:
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=-{\frac {GMm}{r}},}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  G
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  M
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  m
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mi>
                 r
                </mi>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U=-{\frac {GMm}{r}},}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="U=-{\frac {GMm}{r}}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/6e4ba4c6266dadc7bda4935bf42846bd225e4ed1" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:14.481ex; height:5.343ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The negative sign follows the convention that work is gained from a loss of potential energy.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Derivation">
        Derivation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Derivation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Gravitational force between two bodies in space is obtained from the force exerted by a mass
       <i>
        M
       </i>
       on another mass
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =-{\frac {GMm}{r^{3}}}\mathbf {r} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  G
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  M
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  m
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   3
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                r
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =-{\frac {GMm}{r^{3}}}\mathbf {r} ,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\mathbf {F} =-{\frac {GMm}{r^{3}}}\mathbf {r} ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e554fb7c67ee24101094221d989d4502e54f1b56" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:15.484ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <b>
        r
       </b>
       is the position vector from the object with mass
       <i>
        M
       </i>
       to the object with mass
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       , and
       <i>
        G
       </i>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">
        gravitational constant
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       This can also be expressed as
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =-{\frac {GMm}{r^{2}}}\mathbf {\hat {r}} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>

              </msup>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mover>
                 <mi>
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mo>
                  ˙
                  <!-- ˙ -->
                 </mo>
                </mover>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mover>
                  <mi>
                   r
                  </mi>
                  <mo>
                   ˙
                   <!-- ˙ -->
                  </mo>
                 </mover>
                </mrow>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle {\frac {\mathrm {d} }{\mathrm {d} t}}r^{-1}=-r^{-2}{\dot {r}}=-{\frac {\dot {r}}{r^{2}}}.}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" \frac{\mathrm{d}}{\mathrm{d}t}r^{-1}=-r^{-2}\dot{r}=-\frac{\dot{r}}{r^2}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/75e285d2df4bb308fa620792d0f234e183e981bf" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:24.422ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Potential_energy_for_electrostatic_forces_between_two_bodies">
        Potential energy for electrostatic forces between two bodies
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Potential energy for electrostatic forces between two bodies">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The electrostatic force exerted by a charge
       <i>
        Q
       </i>
       on another charge
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} ={\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0}}}{\frac {Qq}{r^{3}}}\mathbf {r} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  4
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  π
                  <!-- π -->
                 </mi>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   ε
                   <!-- ε -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    0
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  Q
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  q
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   3
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                r
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} ={\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0}}}{\frac {Qq}{r^{3}}}\mathbf {r} ,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" \mathbf{F}=\frac{1}{4\pi\varepsilon_0}\frac{Qq}{r^3}\mathbf{r}," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b5cb53c622d5d9edddbbba069ec0e47442e0729b" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:15.742ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <b>
        r
       </b>
       is the position vector from
       <i>
        Q
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ε
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permittivity" title="Vacuum permittivity">
        vacuum permittivity
       </a>
       . This may also be written using
       <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_constant" title="Coulomb's constant">
        Coulomb's constant
       </a>
       <span class="nowrap">
        <i>
         k
        </i>
        <sub>
         e
        </sub>
        = 1 ⁄ 4πε
        <sub>
         0
        </sub>
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       The work
       <i>
        W
       </i>
       required to move
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       from
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       to any point
       <i>
        B
       </i>
       in the electrostatic force field is given by the potential function
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U({r})={\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0}}}{\frac {Qq}{r}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi>
                r
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  4
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  π
                  <!-- π -->
                 </mi>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   ε
                   <!-- ε -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    0
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
