                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  q
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mi>
                 r
                </mi>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U({r})={\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0}}}{\frac {Qq}{r}}.}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="U({r})=\frac{1}{4\pi\varepsilon_0}\frac{Qq}{r}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/95e4b9b3f36a038e27b1aed5baf794475a461320" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:17.598ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Reference_level">
        Reference level
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Reference level">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       The potential energy is a function of the state a system is in, and is defined relative to that for a particular state. This reference state is not always a real state, it may also be a limit, such as with the distances between all bodies tending to infinity, provided that the energy involved in tending to that limit is finite, such as in the case of
       <a href="/wiki/Inverse-square_law" title="Inverse-square law">
        inverse-square law
       </a>
       forces. Any arbitrary reference state could be used, therefore it can be chosen based on convenience.
      </p>
      <p>
       Typically the potential energy of a system depends on the
       <i>
        relative
       </i>
       positions of its components only, so the reference state can also be expressed in terms of relative positions.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Gravitational_potential_energy">
        Gravitational potential energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Gravitational potential energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main articles:
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential" title="Gravitational potential">
        Gravitational potential
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_energy" title="Gravitational energy">
        Gravitational energy
       </a>
       , and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravity_field" title="Gravity field">
        Gravity field
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Gravitational energy is the potential energy associated with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_force" title="Gravitational force">
        gravitational force
       </a>
       , as work is required to elevate objects against Earth's gravity. The potential energy due to elevated positions is called gravitational potential energy, and is evidenced by water in an elevated reservoir or kept behind a dam. If an object falls from one point to another point inside a gravitational field, the force of gravity will do positive work on the object, and the gravitational potential energy will decrease by the same amount.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solar_sys.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="904" data-file-width="1440" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Solar_sys.jpg/220px-Solar_sys.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Solar_sys.jpg/330px-Solar_sys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Solar_sys.jpg/440px-Solar_sys.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solar_sys.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Gravitational force keeps the planets in orbit around the
         <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
          Sun
         </a>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Consider a book placed on top of a table. As the book is raised from the floor, to the table, some external force works against the gravitational force. If the book falls back to the floor, the "falling" energy the book receives is provided by the gravitational force. Thus, if the book falls off the table, this potential energy goes to accelerate the mass of the book and is converted into
       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">
        kinetic energy
       </a>
       . When the book hits the floor this kinetic energy is converted into heat, deformation and sound by the impact.
      </p>
      <p>
       The factors that affect an object's gravitational potential energy are its height relative to some reference point, its mass, and the strength of the gravitational field it is in. Thus, a book lying on a table has less gravitational potential energy than the same book on top of a taller cupboard, and less gravitational potential energy than a heavier book lying on the same table. An object at a certain height above the Moon's surface has less gravitational potential energy than at the same height above the Earth's surface because the Moon's gravity is weaker. Note that "height" in the common sense of the term cannot be used for gravitational potential energy calculations when gravity is not assumed to be a constant. The following sections provide more detail.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Local_approximation">
        Local approximation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Local approximation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The strength of a gravitational field varies with location. However, when the change of distance is small in relation to the distances from the center of the source of the gravitational field, this variation in field strength is negligible and we can assume that the force of gravity on a particular object is constant. Near the surface of the Earth, for example, we assume that the acceleration due to gravity is a constant
       <span class="nowrap">
        <i>
         g
        </i>
        = 9.8 m/s
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </span>
       ("
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravity" title="Standard gravity">
        standard gravity
       </a>
       "). In this case, a simple expression for gravitational potential energy can be derived using the
       <i>
        W
       </i>
       =
       <i>
        Fd
       </i>
       equation for
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mechanical_work" title="Mechanical work">
        work
       </a>
       , and the equation
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle W_{F}=-\Delta U_{F}.\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <msub>
               <mi>
                W
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 F
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               Δ
               <!-- Δ -->
              </mi>
              <msub>
               <mi>
                U
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi>
                 F
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
              <mspace width="negativethinmathspace">
              </mspace>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle W_{F}=-\Delta U_{F}.\!}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="W_F = -\Delta U_F.\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3198898fa402552defce01288b2846a7b6fb82ed" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-right: -0.204ex; width:14.013ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The amount of gravitational potential energy possessed by an elevated object is equal to the work done against gravity in lifting it. The work done equals the force required to move it upward multiplied with the vertical distance it is moved (remember
       <i>
        W = Fd
       </i>
       ). The upward force required while moving at a constant velocity is equal to the weight,
       <i>
        mg
       </i>
       , of an object, so the work done in lifting it through a height
       <i>
        h
       </i>
       is the product
       <i>
        mgh
       </i>
       . Thus, when accounting only for
       <a href="/wiki/Mass" title="Mass">
        mass
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">
        gravity
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Altitude" title="Altitude">
        altitude
       </a>
       , the equation is:
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=mgh\!}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mi>
               g
              </mi>
              <mi>
               h
              </mi>
              <mspace width="negativethinmathspace">
              </mspace>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U=mgh\!}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="U = mgh\!" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/cc083357315c934fd442a9be32920bca1fb57f98" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; margin-right: -0.338ex; width:9.328ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       is the potential energy of the object relative to its being on the Earth's surface,
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       is the mass of the object,
       <i>
        g
       </i>
       is the acceleration due to gravity, and
       <i>
        h
       </i>
       is the altitude of the object.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       If
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       is expressed in
       <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">
        kilograms
       </a>
       ,
       <i>
        g
       </i>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second_squared" title="Metre per second squared">
        m/s
        <sup>
         2
        </sup>
       </a>
       and
       <i>
        h
       </i>
       in
       <a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">
        metres
       </a>
       then
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       will be calculated in
       <a href="/wiki/Joule" title="Joule">
        joules
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Hence, the potential difference is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \,\Delta U=mg\Delta h.\ }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mspace width="thinmathspace">
              </mspace>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               Δ
               <!-- Δ -->
              </mi>
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mi>
               g
              </mi>
              <mi mathvariant="normal">
               Δ
               <!-- Δ -->
              </mi>
              <mi>
               h
              </mi>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
              <mtext>
              </mtext>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \,\Delta U=mg\Delta h.\ }
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="\,\Delta U = mg \Delta h.\ " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d649ae09dd41946d17cd1069b5995a9806c4b3ed" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:15.25ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="General_formula">
        General formula
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: General formula">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       However, over large variations in distance, the approximation that
       <i>
        g
       </i>
       is constant is no longer valid, and we have to use
       <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">
        calculus
       </a>
       and the general mathematical definition of work to determine gravitational potential energy. For the computation of the potential energy we can
       <a href="/wiki/Integral" title="Integral">
        integrate
       </a>
       the gravitational force, whose magnitude is given by
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Law of universal gravitation">
        Newton's law of gravitation
       </a>
       , with respect to the distance
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       between the two bodies. Using that definition, the gravitational potential energy of a system of masses
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       <sub>
        1
       </sub>
       and
       <i>
        M
       </i>
       <sub>
        2
       </sub>
       at a distance
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       using
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">
        gravitational constant
       </a>
       <i>
        G
       </i>
       is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=-G{\frac {m_{1}M_{2}}{r}}\ +K}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               G
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   m
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    1
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   M
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mrow>
                <mi>
                 r
                </mi>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mtext>
              </mtext>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mi>
               K
              </mi>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U=-G{\frac {m_{1}M_{2}}{r}}\ +K}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="U = -G \frac{m_1 M_2}{r}\ + K" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e3979a6d83379b2c19d4a97870dfe3e7edbbda4d" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:21.242ex; height:5.176ex;"/>
        </span>
        ,
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <i>
        K
       </i>
       is an arbitrary constant dependent on the choice of datum from which potential is measured. Choosing the convention that
       <i>
        K
       </i>
       =0 (i.e. in relation to a point at infinity) makes calculations simpler, albeit at the cost of making
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       negative; for why this is physically reasonable, see below.
      </p>
      <p>
       Given this formula for
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       , the total potential energy of a system of
       <i>
        n
       </i>
       bodies is found by summing, for all
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\frac {n(n-1)}{2}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mfrac>
               <mrow>
                <mi>
                 n
                </mi>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 (
                </mo>
                <mi>
                 n
                </mi>
                <mo>
                 −
                 <!-- − -->
                </mo>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mo stretchy="false">
                 )
                </mo>
               </mrow>
               <mn>
                2
               </mn>
              </mfrac>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {\frac {n(n-1)}{2}}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\frac{n ( n - 1 )}{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4d7e4c7b0026c4729031e5aa8110d5bb700d0162" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:9.438ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       pairs of two bodies, the potential energy of the system of those two bodies.
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gravitational_potential_summation_2.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="448" data-file-width="592" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Gravitational_potential_summation_2.png/220px-Gravitational_potential_summation_2.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Gravitational_potential_summation_2.png/330px-Gravitational_potential_summation_2.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Gravitational_potential_summation_2.png/440px-Gravitational_potential_summation_2.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gravitational_potential_summation_2.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Gravitational potential summation
         <span class="mwe-math-element">
          <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
           <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=-m(G{\frac {M_{1}}{r_{1}}}+G{\frac {M_{2}}{r_{2}}})}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
            <semantics>
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
               <mi>
                U
               </mi>
               <mo>
                =
               </mo>
               <mo>
                −
                <!-- − -->
               </mo>
               <mi>
                m
               </mi>
               <mo stretchy="false">
                (
               </mo>
               <mi>
                G
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mfrac>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   M
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    1
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   r
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    1
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mfrac>
               </mrow>
               <mo>
                +
               </mo>
               <mi>
                G
               </mi>
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mfrac>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   M
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   r
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    2
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mfrac>
               </mrow>
               <mo stretchy="false">
                )
               </mo>
              </mstyle>
             </mrow>
             <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
              {\displaystyle U=-m(G{\frac {M_{1}}{r_{1}}}+G{\frac {M_{2}}{r_{2}}})}
             </annotation>
            </semantics>
           </math>
          </span>
          <img alt="U = - m (G \frac{ M_1}{r_1}+ G \frac{ M_2}{r_2}) " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/bae298b389dad83eaa5eed7640e9975715fbbfa0" style="vertical-align: -2.171ex; width:25.322ex; height:5.509ex;"/>
         </span>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Considering the system of bodies as the combined set of small particles the bodies consist of, and applying the previous on the particle level we get the negative
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_binding_energy" title="Gravitational binding energy">
        gravitational binding energy
       </a>
       . This potential energy is more strongly negative than the total potential energy of the system of bodies as such since it also includes the negative gravitational binding energy of each body. The potential energy of the system of bodies as such is the negative of the energy needed to separate the bodies from each other to infinity, while the gravitational binding energy is the energy needed to separate all particles from each other to infinity.
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=-m\left(G{\frac {M_{1}}{r_{1}}}+G{\frac {M_{2}}{r_{2}}}\right)}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mrow>
               <mo>
                (
               </mo>
               <mrow>
                <mi>
                 G
                </mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mfrac>
                  <msub>
                   <mi>
                    M
                   </mi>
                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mn>
                     1
                    </mn>
                   </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <msub>
                   <mi>
                    r
                   </mi>
                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mn>
                     1
                    </mn>
                   </mrow>
                  </msub>
                 </mfrac>
                </mrow>
                <mo>
                 +
                </mo>
                <mi>
                 G
                </mi>
                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                 <mfrac>
                  <msub>
                   <mi>
                    M
                   </mi>
                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mn>
                     2
                    </mn>
                   </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <msub>
                   <mi>
                    r
                   </mi>
                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                    <mn>
                     2
                    </mn>
                   </mrow>
                  </msub>
                 </mfrac>
                </mrow>
               </mrow>
               <mo>
                )
               </mo>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U=-m\left(G{\frac {M_{1}}{r_{1}}}+G{\frac {M_{2}}{r_{2}}}\right)}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="U = - m \left(G \frac{ M_1}{r_1}+ G \frac{ M_2}{r_2}\right) " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/8425aefd5785206e19b574034ece0eee18084f0f" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:27.321ex; height:6.176ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       therefore,
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=-m\sum G{\frac {M}{r}}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               m
              </mi>
              <mo>
               ∑
               <!-- ∑ -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               G
              </mi>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mi>
                 M
                </mi>
                <mi>
                 r
                </mi>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U=-m\sum G{\frac {M}{r}}}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="U = - m  \sum G \frac{ M}{r}  " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a0f95f1a4c96f76cf3c4e22454bf2d5570772842" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:17.964ex; height:5.176ex;"/>
        </span>
        ,
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h3>
       <span id="Why_choose_a_convention_where_gravitational_energy_is_negative.3F">
       </span>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Why_choose_a_convention_where_gravitational_energy_is_negative?">
        Why choose a convention where gravitational energy is negative?
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Why choose a convention where gravitational energy is negative?">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       As with all potential energies, only differences in gravitational potential energy matter for most physical purposes, and the choice of zero point is arbitrary. Given that there is no reasonable criterion for preferring one particular finite
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       over another, there seem to be only two reasonable choices for the distance at which
       <i>
        U
       </i>
       becomes zero:
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle r=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              r
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle r=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="r=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/894a83e863728b4ee2e12f3a999a09f5f2bf1c89" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.31ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle r=\infty }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              r
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mi mathvariant="normal">
              ∞
              <!-- ∞ -->
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle r=\infty }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="r=\infty" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d38c8164ddb69351cdab28da290255fde3b846d4" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:6.471ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       . The choice of
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              U
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle U=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="U=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d868ee7902023d29169252eb059f8faff9f08fc1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:6.044ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       at infinity may seem peculiar, and the consequence that gravitational energy is always negative may seem counterintuitive, but this choice allows gravitational potential energy values to be finite, albeit negative.
      </p>
      <p>
       The
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathematical_singularity" title="Mathematical singularity">
        singularity
       </a>
       at
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle r=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              r
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle r=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="r=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/894a83e863728b4ee2e12f3a999a09f5f2bf1c89" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.31ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       in the formula for gravitational potential energy means that the only other apparently reasonable alternative choice of convention, with
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              U
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle U=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="U=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d868ee7902023d29169252eb059f8faff9f08fc1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:6.044ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       for
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle r=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              r
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle r=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="r=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/894a83e863728b4ee2e12f3a999a09f5f2bf1c89" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:5.31ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       , would result in potential energy being positive, but infinitely large for all nonzero values of
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       , and would make calculations involving sums or differences of potential energies beyond what is possible with the
       <a href="/wiki/Real_number" title="Real number">
        real number
       </a>
       system. Since physicists abhor infinities in their calculations, and
       <i>
        r
       </i>
       is always non-zero in practice, the choice of
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=0}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              U
             </mi>
             <mo>
              =
             </mo>
             <mn>
              0
             </mn>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle U=0}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="U=0" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d868ee7902023d29169252eb059f8faff9f08fc1" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:6.044ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       at infinity is by far the more preferable choice, even if the idea of negative energy in a
       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_well" title="Gravity well">
        gravity well
       </a>
       appears to be peculiar at first.
      </p>
      <p>
       The negative value for gravitational energy also has deeper implications that make it seem more reasonable in cosmological calculations where the total energy of the universe can meaningfully be considered; see
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inflation_theory" title="Inflation theory">
        inflation theory
       </a>
       for more on this.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">
        Uses
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Uses">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Gravitational potential energy has a number of practical uses, notably the generation of
       <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">
        pumped-storage hydroelectricity
       </a>
       . For example, in
       <a href="/wiki/Dinorwig_Power_Station" title="Dinorwig Power Station">
        Dinorwig
       </a>
       , Wales, there are two lakes, one at a higher elevation than the other. At times when surplus electricity is not required (and so is comparatively cheap), water is pumped up to the higher lake, thus converting the electrical energy (running the pump) to gravitational potential energy. At times of peak demand for electricity, the water flows back down through electrical generator turbines, converting the potential energy into kinetic energy and then back into electricity. The process is not completely efficient and some of the original energy from the surplus electricity is in fact lost to friction.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EconomistPSH_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-EconomistPSH-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thier_11-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-thier-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Levine_12-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Levine-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-yang_13-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-yang-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-heco_14-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-heco-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Gravitational potential energy is also used to power clocks in which falling weights operate the mechanism.
      </p>
      <div style="clear:right;">
      </div>
      <p>
       It's also used by
       <a href="/wiki/Counterweight" title="Counterweight">
        counterweights
       </a>
       for lifting up an
       <a href="/wiki/Elevator" title="Elevator">
        elevator
       </a>
       , crane, or
       <a href="/wiki/Sash_window" title="Sash window">
        sash window
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rollercoasters" title="Rollercoasters">
        Roller coasters
       </a>
       are an entertaining way to utilize potential energy - chains are used to move a car up an incline (building up gravitational potential energy), to then have that energy converted into kinetic energy as it falls.
      </p>
      <p>
       Another practical use is utilizing gravitational potential energy to descend (perhaps coast) downhill in transportation such as the descent of an automobile, truck, railroad train, bicycle, airplane, or fluid in a pipeline. In some cases the
       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">
        kinetic energy
       </a>
       obtained from potential energy of descent may be used to start ascending the next grade such as what happens when a road is undulating and has frequent dips. The commercialization of stored energy (in the form of rail cars raised to higher elevations) that is then converted to electrical energy when needed by an electrical grid, is being undertaken in the United States in a system called
       <a href="/wiki/List_of_energy_storage_projects#Gravitational_potential_energy_storage" title="List of energy storage projects">
        Advanced Rail Energy Storage
       </a>
       (ARES).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Economist-2012.03.03_15-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Economist-2012.03.03-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bloomberg-2012.09.06_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Bloomberg-2012.09.06-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <i>
         Further information:
         <a href="/wiki/List_of_energy_storage_projects#Gravitational_potential_energy_storage" title="List of energy storage projects">
          Gravitational potential energy storage
         </a>
        </i>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Chemical_potential_energy">
        Chemical potential energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Chemical potential energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_energy" title="Chemical energy">
        Chemical energy
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Chemical potential energy is a form of potential energy related to the structural arrangement of atoms or molecules. This arrangement may be the result of
       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_bond" title="Chemical bond">
        chemical bonds
       </a>
       within a molecule or otherwise. Chemical energy of a chemical substance can be transformed to other forms of energy by a
       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_reaction" title="Chemical reaction">
        chemical reaction
       </a>
       . As an example, when a fuel is burned the chemical energy is converted to heat, same is the case with digestion of food metabolized in a biological organism. Green plants transform
       <a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">
        solar energy
       </a>
       to chemical energy through the process known as
       <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">
        photosynthesis
       </a>
       , and electrical energy can be converted to chemical energy through
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electrochemical" title="Electrochemical">
        electrochemical
       </a>
       reactions.
      </p>
      <p>
       The similar term
       <a href="/wiki/Chemical_potential" title="Chemical potential">
        chemical potential
       </a>
       is used to indicate the potential of a substance to undergo a change of configuration, be it in the form of a chemical reaction, spatial transport, particle exchange with a reservoir, etc.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electric_potential_energy">
        Electric potential energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Electric potential energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential_energy" title="Electric potential energy">
        Electric potential energy
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       An object can have potential energy by virtue of its
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_charge" title="Electric charge">
        electric charge
       </a>
       and several forces related to their presence. There are two main types of this kind of potential energy: electrostatic potential energy, electrodynamic potential energy (also sometimes called magnetic potential energy).
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plasma-lamp_2.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1609" data-file-width="1589" height="223" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Plasma-lamp_2.jpg/220px-Plasma-lamp_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Plasma-lamp_2.jpg/330px-Plasma-lamp_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Plasma-lamp_2.jpg/440px-Plasma-lamp_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plasma-lamp_2.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         <a href="/wiki/Plasma_(physics)" title="Plasma (physics)">
          Plasma
         </a>
         formed inside a gas filled sphere
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Electrostatic_potential_energy">
        Electrostatic potential energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Electrostatic potential energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Electrostatic potential energy between two bodies in space is obtained from the force exerted by a charge
       <i>
        Q
       </i>
       on another charge
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       which is given by
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =-{\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0}}}{\frac {Qq}{r^{2}}}\mathbf {r} ,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                F
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  4
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  π
                  <!-- π -->
                 </mi>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   ε
                   <!-- ε -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    0
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  Q
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  q
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <msup>
                 <mi>
                  r
                 </mi>
                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                  <mn>
                   2
                  </mn>
                 </mrow>
                </msup>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                r
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =-{\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0}}}{\frac {Qq}{r^{2}}}\mathbf {r} ,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle \mathbf {F} =-{\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0}}}{\frac {Qq}{r^{2}}}\mathbf {r} ,}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/aa2411b8c1b793e4188414b52a9c2ec9bdfb973d" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:17.551ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where
       <b>
        r
       </b>
       is the position vector from
       <i>
        Q
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        ε
       </i>
       <sub>
        0
       </sub>
       is the
       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum_permittivity" title="Vacuum permittivity">
        vacuum permittivity
       </a>
       . This may also be written using
       <a href="/wiki/Coulomb%27s_constant" title="Coulomb's constant">
        Coulomb's constant
       </a>
       <span class="nowrap">
        <i>
         k
        </i>
        <sub>
         e
        </sub>
        = 1 ⁄ 4πε
        <sub>
         0
        </sub>
       </span>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       If the electric charge of an object can be assumed to be at rest, then it has potential energy due to its position relative to other charged objects. The
       <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential_energy" title="Electric potential energy">
        electrostatic potential energy
       </a>
       is the energy of an electrically charged particle (at rest) in an electric field. It is defined as the
       <a href="/wiki/Work_(physics)" title="Work (physics)">
        work
       </a>
       that must be done to move it from an infinite distance away to its present location, adjusted for non-electrical forces on the object. This energy will generally be non-zero if there is another electrically charged object nearby.
      </p>
      <p>
       The work
       <i>
        W
       </i>
       required to move
       <i>
        q
       </i>
       from
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       to any point
       <i>
        B
       </i>
       in the electrostatic force field is given by the potential function
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U({\mathbf {r}})={\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0}}}{\frac {Qq}{r}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                <mi mathvariant="bold">
                 r
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </mrow>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mrow>
                 <mn>
                  4
                 </mn>
                 <mi>
                  π
                  <!-- π -->
                 </mi>
                 <msub>
                  <mi>
                   ε
                   <!-- ε -->
                  </mi>
                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
                   <mn>
                    0
                   </mn>
                  </mrow>
                 </msub>
                </mrow>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mrow>
                 <mi>
                  Q
                 </mi>
                 <mi>
                  q
                 </mi>
                </mrow>
                <mi>
                 r
                </mi>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U({\mathbf {r}})={\frac {1}{4\pi \varepsilon _{0}}}{\frac {Qq}{r}}.}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="U(\bold{r})=\frac{1}{4\pi\varepsilon_0}\frac{Qq}{r}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7766b6a112b935641416f9d02d9ce3eb794d9e40" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:17.652ex; height:5.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       A related quantity called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Electric_potential" title="Electric potential">
         electric potential
        </a>
       </i>
       (commonly denoted with a
       <i>
        V
       </i>
       for voltage) is equal to the electric potential energy per unit charge.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Magnetic_potential_energy">
        Magnetic potential energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Magnetic potential energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The energy of a
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_moment" title="Magnetic moment">
        magnetic moment
       </a>
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\mu }}}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
              <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
               μ
               <!-- μ -->
              </mi>
             </mrow>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle {\boldsymbol {\mu }}}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="{\boldsymbol {\mu }}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a1aee7d7b4a36d96dfb35bfee9c7751bba1fdfbe" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.646ex; height:2.009ex;"/>
       </span>
       in an externally produced
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">
        magnetic B-field
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         B
        </b>
       </span>
       has potential energy
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
        <a href="#cite_note-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=-{\boldsymbol {\mu }}\cdot \mathbf {B} .}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold-italic">
                μ
                <!-- μ -->
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               .
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U=-{\boldsymbol {\mu }}\cdot \mathbf {B} .}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="{\displaystyle U=-{\boldsymbol {\mu }}\cdot \mathbf {B} .}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5f207b3923b54bab1f029d006f1d8ace1d52f3f1" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:12.562ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Magnetization" title="Magnetization">
        magnetization
       </a>
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       in a field is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U=-{\frac {1}{2}}\int \mathbf {M} \cdot \mathbf {B} \mathrm {d} V,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
           <semantics>
            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
              <mi>
               U
              </mi>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mfrac>
                <mn>
                 1
                </mn>
                <mn>
                 2
                </mn>
               </mfrac>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ∫
               <!-- ∫ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                M
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mo>
               ⋅
               <!-- ⋅ -->
              </mo>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="bold">
                B
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
               <mi mathvariant="normal">
                d
               </mi>
              </mrow>
              <mi>
               V
              </mi>
              <mo>
               ,
              </mo>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U=-{\frac {1}{2}}\int \mathbf {M} \cdot \mathbf {B} \mathrm {d} V,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt=" U = -\frac{1}{2}\int \mathbf{M}\cdot\mathbf{B} \mathrm{d}V, " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/0298b248117aea630638dff55d92b9d96ff8bb84" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:21.5ex; height:5.676ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       where the integral can be over all space or, equivalently, where
       <span class="texhtml">
        <b>
         M
        </b>
       </span>
       is nonzero.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Magnetic potential energy is the form of energy related not only to the distance between magnetic materials, but also to the orientation, or alignment, of those materials within the field. For example, the needle of a compass has the lowest magnetic potential energy when it is aligned with the north and south poles of the Earth's magnetic field. If the needle is moved by an outside force, torque is exerted on the magnetic dipole of the needle by the Earth's magnetic field, causing it to move back into alignment. The magnetic potential energy of the needle is highest when its field is in the same direction as the Earth's magnetic field. Two magnets will have potential energy in relation to each other and the distance between them, but this also depends on their orientation. If the opposite poles are held apart, the potential energy will be higher the further they are apart and lower the closer they are. Conversely, like poles will have the highest potential energy when forced together, and the lowest when they spring apart.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
        <a href="#cite_note-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
        <a href="#cite_note-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Nuclear_potential_energy">
        Nuclear potential energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nuclear potential energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Nuclear potential energy is the potential energy of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_particles" title="Nuclear particles">
        particles
       </a>
       inside an
       <a href="/wiki/Atomic_nucleus" title="Atomic nucleus">
        atomic nucleus
       </a>
       . The nuclear particles are bound together by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Strong_nuclear_force" title="Strong nuclear force">
        strong nuclear force
       </a>
       .
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_nuclear_force" title="Weak nuclear force">
        Weak nuclear forces
       </a>
       provide the potential energy for certain kinds of radioactive decay, such as
       <a href="/wiki/Beta_decay" title="Beta decay">
        beta decay
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Nuclear particles like protons and neutrons are not destroyed in fission and fusion processes, but collections of them can have less mass than if they were individually free, in which case this mass difference can be liberated as heat and radiation in nuclear reactions (the heat and radiation have the missing mass, but it often escapes from the system, where it is not measured). The energy from the
       <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">
        Sun
       </a>
       is an example of this form of energy conversion. In the Sun, the process of hydrogen fusion converts about 4 million tonnes of solar matter per second into
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_energy" title="Electromagnetic energy">
        electromagnetic energy
       </a>
       , which is radiated into space.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Forces_and_potential_energy">
        Forces and potential energy
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Potential_energy&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Forces and potential energy">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Potential energy is closely linked with
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Force_(physics)" title="Force (physics)">
        forces
       </a>
       . If the work done by a force on a body that moves from
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       to
       <i>
        B
       </i>
       does not depend on the path between these points, then the work of this force measured from
       <i>
        A
       </i>
       assigns a scalar value to every other point in space and defines a
       <a href="/wiki/Scalar_potential" title="Scalar potential">
        scalar potential
       </a>
       field. In this case, the force can be defined as the negative of the
       <a href="/wiki/Gradient" title="Gradient">
        vector gradient
       </a>
       of the potential field.
      </p>
      <p>
       For example, gravity is a
       <a href="/wiki/Conservative_force" title="Conservative force">
        conservative force
       </a>
       . The associated potential is the
       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_potential" title="Gravitational potential">
        gravitational potential
       </a>
       , often denoted by
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \phi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              ϕ
              <!-- ϕ -->
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \phi }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\phi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/72b1f30316670aee6270a28334bdf4f5072cdde4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.385ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       or
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle V}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>

             </mi>

          </semantics>

       </span>
       , corresponding to the energy per unit mass as a function of position. The gravitational potential energy of two particles of mass
       <i>
        M
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        m
       </i>
       separated by a distance
       <i>
        r
       </i>

      </p>

            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">

        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>

      <p>
       where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">

      <p>
       The work done against gravity by moving an

       </span>
       to point B with
       <span class="mwe-math-element">

       </span>
       is
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">

       </span>

       <span class="mwe-math-element">

       </span>
       so that the total work done in moving from A to B and returning to A is

        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       If the potential is redefined at A to be
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle a+c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

              c
             </mi>

       </span>
       and the potential at B to be
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle b+c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

             </mo>

             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>

       </span>
       , where
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle c}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="c" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/86a67b81c2de995bd608d5b2df50cd8cd7d92455" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.007ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       is a constant (i.e.
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle c}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              c
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle c}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="c" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/86a67b81c2de995bd608d5b2df50cd8cd7d92455" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.007ex; height:1.676ex;"/>
       </span>
       can be any number, positive or negative, but it must be the same at A as it is at B) then the work done going from A to B is
      </p>
      <dl>
       <dd>
        <span class="mwe-math-element">
         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
          <math alttext="{\displaystyle U_{A\to B}=(b+c)-(a+c)=b-a\,}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

                </mi>

                 <!-- → -->
                </mo>
                <mi>
                 B
                </mi>
               </mrow>
              </msub>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mi>
               b
              </mi>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mi>
               c
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               −
               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               (
              </mo>
              <mi>
               a
              </mi>
              <mo>
               +
              </mo>
              <mi>
               c
              </mi>
              <mo stretchy="false">
               )
              </mo>
              <mo>
               =
              </mo>
              <mi>
               b
              </mi>
              <mo>

               <!-- − -->
              </mo>
              <mi>
               a

              </mspace>
             </mstyle>
            </mrow>
            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
             {\displaystyle U_{A\to B}=(b+c)-(a+c)=b-a\,}
            </annotation>
           </semantics>
          </math>
         </span>
         <img alt="U_{A \to B} = (b + c) - (a + c) = b - a \," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/40e4e51db1c3fdcd132b71763e84d6cbd73a5f90" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:33.975ex; height:2.843ex;"/>
        </span>
       </dd>
      </dl>
      <p>
       as before.
      </p>
      <p>
       In practical terms, this means that one can set the zero of
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle U}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              U
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle U}
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="U" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/458a728f53b9a0274f059cd695e067c430956025" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.783ex; height:2.176ex;"/>
       </span>
       and
       <span class="mwe-math-element">
        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">
         <math alttext="{\displaystyle \phi }" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">
          <semantics>
           <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">
            <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">
             <mi>
              ϕ
              <!-- ϕ -->
             </mi>
            </mstyle>
           </mrow>
           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">
            {\displaystyle \phi }
           </annotation>
          </semantics>
         </math>
        </span>
        <img alt="\phi " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/72b1f30316670aee6270a28334bdf4f5072cdde4" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:1.385ex; height:2.509ex;"/>
       </span>
       anywhere one likes. One may set it to be zero at the surface of the
       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">
        Earth
       </a>
       , or may find it more convenient to set zero at infinity (as in the expressions given earlier in this section).
      </p>
      <p>
       A conservative force can be expressed in the language of
       <a href="/wiki/Differential_geometry" title="Differential geometry">
        differential geometry
       </a>
       as a
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Closed_differential_form" title="Closed differential form">
        closed form
       </a>
       . As Euclidean space is
       <a href="/wiki/Contractible_space" title="Contractible space">
        contractible
       </a>
       , its
       <a href="/wiki/De_Rham_cohomology" title="De Rham cohomology">
        de Rham cohomology
       </a>
       vanishes, so every closed form is also an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Exact_differential_form" title="Exact differential form">
        exact form
       </a>
       , and can be expressed as the gradient of a scalar field. This gives a mathematical justification of the fact that all conservative forces are gradients of a potential field.
      </p>
